<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2018, <strong>10</strong>, 13277-13288</div><h1 id="sect202"><span class="title_heading">New insights into the electrochemistry of magnesium molybdate hierarchical architectures for high performance sodium devices<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Manickam 
            Minakshi
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Manickam&nbsp;Minakshi (orcid.org/0000-0001-6558-8317) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6558-8317"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">ab</span></sup>,

            Munnangi

      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Engineering and Information Technology, Murdoch University, WA 6150, Australia. E-mail: <a href="mailto:minakshi@murdoch.edu.au">minakshi@murdoch.edu.au</a></span>

      <br><a id="affd"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">d</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Centre for Materials and Surface Science, La Trobe University, Bundoora, VIC 3086, Australia</span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      11th May 2018
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 12th June 2018</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 12th June 2018</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Magnesium molybdate (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>), which possesses synergistic features combining both hierarchical plate-like nanomaterials and porous architectures, has been successfully synthesized through a facile combustion synthesis at a low temperature. The hierarchical architecture is characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM), scanning transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses. The as-obtained MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanoplates showed a porous structure with a pore-size distribution ranging from 50 to 70 nm. This porosity provides an electron transport pathway and enhanced surface reaction kinetics. The binding energies measured for Mg 2p, Mo 3d, 3p and O 1s are consistent with the literature, and with the metal ions being present as M(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) and M(<span class="small_caps">VI</span>) states, respectively. This indicates that the oxidation states of the metal cations are as expected. The electrochemical behaviour of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was investigated using aqueous (NaOH) and non-aqueous solvents (NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC) for supercapacitor and battery applications. The sodium-ion capacitor involves ion absorption and insertion into the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrodes resulting in superior power and energy densities. However, the cycling stability was found to be stable only for an aqueous system. The formation of a solid electrolyte surface layer restricted the reversible capacity of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the sodium-battery. Nevertheless, it does offer some promise as an anode material for storing energy with high rate performance and excellent capacity retention. Detailed comparative analyses of various electrolytes in storage devices such as hybrid sodium-ion capacitors and sodium-ion batteries are vital for the integration of hierarchical structured materials into practical applications. The reaction mechanisms are postulated.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect283"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Today, the most significant global challenge is energy. It is reported that almost 80% of the world's energy consumption is from non-renewable sources.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Issues surrounding the use of fossil fuels and their environmental impacts have resulted in considerable interest in renewable energy. The intermittent nature of most forms of renewable energy (solar, wind, tidal) dictates that energy storage is a critical part of a reduced reliance on dispatchable sources of energy such as gas and coal. Batteries, supercapacitors, and hydrogen storage systems have great potential in efficient renewable energy storage.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,2</span></sup></a> Among different types of energy storage devices being examined, electrochemical energy storage devices are the most promising candidates.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3,4</span></sup></a> The current rechargeable batteries based on lithium- or sodium-ions need to be optimized for storing renewable energy. This is due to relatively low power density and limited cyclability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">One approach to improving the energy storage technology involves combining elements of both batteries and capacitors in a device termed a hybrid capacitor.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> This delivers high energy and power density as well as reasonably long cycling stability with fast charging/discharging capabilities. However, hybrid capacitors deliver a much lower energy density compared to that of batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5,6</span></sup></a> Therefore, the current work has been focussed on the improvement of energy density <span class="italic">via</span> rational design of materials and using a facile synthesis approach to obtain an electrode with the desired characteristics. Nanomaterials with a particle size distribution ranging from tens to hundreds of nanometers are found to be suitable for ion absorption, faster surface redox reaction, and ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7</span></sup></a> In this work, we have developed and investigated a novel form of magnesium molybdate (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>), having both a hierarchical architecture and porous structure. The use of a porous electrode helps alleviate the internal stress created during rapid charging and discharging. The hierarchically porous material also offers high capacitance and rapid ion transport owing to its low resistance to electrolyte diffusion. This structure is likely to offer considerable advantages to the electrochemical performances in the molybdate family of materials. Combining such hierarchical structures to fabricate nanoplate electrodes might provide advanced materials with enhanced performance.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Various transition metal cations have been reported in the binary metal oxide framework AMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (A = divalent metal ions; <span class="italic">i.e.</span> Mn, Co, and Ni).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">812</span></sup></a> The specific capacitance for the hybrid device of the nanostructured MnMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> CoMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> and NiMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> was reported to be around 20, 25 and 70 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> when performed in a two-electrode cell at 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> over 1000 cycles with a voltage window of 1.6 V. However, the capacity retention of the NiMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode is shown to deteriorate over multiple cycles.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,13</span></sup></a> Though the binary metal oxide framework has been used as an electrode in lithium-ion batteries, it is not widely reported for sodium systems.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we report the synthesis and electrochemistry of magnesium molybdate (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) hierarchical architectures obtained through a combustion route. We have assembled a hybrid capacitor based on MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as the positive electrode and activated carbon as the negative electrode. Electrolytes tested included NaOH solution and a non-aqueous solvent (NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC) separately. Many aqueous electrolytes such as acid, neutral and alkaline media have been explored as electrolytes in both batteries and supercapacitors. However, the corrosive nature of sulphate ions limits the cycling stability of the energy storage devices. Therefore, to have a meaningful comparison, NaOH has been chosen. To the best of our knowledge, there is no work reported on MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> for hybrid devices. We have tested and compared their performances in both aqueous and non-aqueous electrolytes. In both cases, the specific capacitance was around 43 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The cell could be cycled reversibly in a voltage region of 1.6 V (aqueous) and 3.0 V (non-aqueous) but the capacitance retention is found to be excellent only for the aqueous system. In this aqueous case, plate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> exhibiting a capacitance of 43 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with an excellent cycling retention of over 95% in 1000 cycles is reported. In order to develop an alternative anode material, which can rival the hard carbon anode used in sodium-ion batteries, we extended our studies to test the feasibility of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in a battery using MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as the positive electrode and metallic sodium as the negative electrode. The preliminary results showed that MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> is suitable for high cycling stability with excellent retention (95% over 75 cycles) in discharge capacity. However, the storage capacity of 225 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> needs to be improved for practical applications. Our physical and electrochemical approaches provide an understanding of the electrochemistry, the redox mechanism, the role of the nanostructured electrode material and the type of electrolyte on the performance of this system. The chargedischarge mechanisms of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the sodium-ion battery have also been postulated. The insights gained should enable us to further enhance and develop the storage capability of this type of system.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect323"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect325"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of magnesium molybdate</span></h3>
        <span>Magnesium molybdate (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) was synthesised using the combustion process. All reagents were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. In a typical synthesis, stoichiometric amounts of magnesium nitrate (Mg(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) in the presence of ammonium molybdate tetrahydrate ((NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>6</sub></small>Mo<small><sub>7</sub></small>O<small><sub>24</sub></small>4H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) and urea (CO(NH<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>) as a fuel were dissolved in de-ionised water (about 50 mL) with magnetic stirring. The pH of the solution was adjusted to 7 by dropwise addition of ammonia solution. Subsequently, the solution was evaporated on a heater overnight and transferred to an oven at 150 C for 2 h for complete dehydration of the precursors. Once dried, the dehydrated sample was transferred into a furnace at 300 C for 3 hours to transform the precursors into plate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> hierarchical architectures.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect340"><span class="b_heading">Physical characterisation of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small></span></h3>
        <span>The phase purity of the as-synthesized sample was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Philips PANalytical, Bruker D8) analysis using Cu-K radiation at an accelerating voltage and current of 40 kV and 30 mA, respectively.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The morphology of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> material was characterized using a Mira VP  Field Emission Scanning Electron Microscope (FESEM). The high-resolution imaging of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> material was carried out <span class="italic">via</span> scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) imaging in various imaging modes: high angle annular dark field imaging (HAADF), bright field (BF) and secondary electron (SE) using a JEOL ARM 200F TEM operating at 200 kV. STEM specimens were prepared by grinding a small amount of powder in methanol and dispersing on a holey carbon film.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Brunauer, Emmett, and Tellet (BET) surface area measurements and porosity analysis were also carried out using a Micromeritics Tristar II surface area and a porosity analyser. For porosity measurements, all samples were degassed at 100 C overnight prior to analysis.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectra were acquired using a Kratos AXIS Nova instrument (Kratos Analytical Ltd, UK) equipped with a monochromated Al K radiation source (1486.69 eV) operating at 150 W power (15 kV, 10 mA). The source was operated at 150 W power (15 kV, 10 mA). The analysis chamber pressure was &lt;1  10<small><sup>9</sup></small> Torr at the beginning of the analysis. High-resolution spectra acquired for selected photoemissions were recorded at 0.1 eV per step and a pass energy of 20 eV. The analysis area was approx. 300 m  700 m. All spectra are calibrated by setting the main O 1s component position to 530.45 eV.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect351"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical characterisation of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small></span></h3>
        <span>Electrolytes used were aqueous 2 M NaOH and non-aqueous 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC (88<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2) for all supercapacitor and sodium-ion battery measurements. The hybrid device was constructed with MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as the positive electrode and conventional activated carbon (AC) as the negative electrode. For the sodium-ion battery, metallic sodium (from Sigma Aldrich) was used as the anode.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">For the three-electrode tests, a platinum wire of 10 cm length and 1 mm diameter and mercurymercuric oxide (Hg/HgO) served as the counter and reference electrodes, respectively. For electrochemical measurements, the materials were investigated by constructing a working electrode consisting of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (or) activated carbon (70 wt%), carbon black (20 wt%), and poly-vinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder (10 wt%) with 0.4 mL of <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidine (NMP) to make a slurry. Ingredients were mixed in an agate mortar to produce a uniform paste, which was coated onto a 1 cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> graphite sheet/stainless steel disc as a current collector. Galvanostatic chargedischarge experiments and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy were performed using an SP-150, Bio-Logic instrument and an Arbin battery testing unit (ARBIN BT-2000). Chargedischarge studies were carried out at a current density of 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> unless otherwise stated. All the measurements relating to the non-aqueous electrolyte involved a sealed Swagelok-type cell which was assembled in a glovebox under an Ar atmosphere and the analysis was then carried out in air in a similar manner to that used for the aqueous system but with different negative electrodes.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The specific capacitance and energy density of the hybrid device were calculated at the end of each chargedischarge test. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was carried out with an amplitude of 5 mV over a frequency range of 10 mHz to 700 kHz at an open circuit potential. The specific capacitance (SC) and energy density (<span class="italic">E</span>) of the device were calculated using the following equations:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1">SC (F g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) = <span class="italic">I</span><span class="italic">t</span>/<span class="italic">m</span><span class="italic">V</span></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2"><span class="italic">E</span> (W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>) = <span class="italic">E</span> = SC<span class="italic">V</span><small><sup>2</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>where SC is the specific capacitance (F g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), <span class="italic">I</span> is the current (A) imposed to the cell for charge/discharge, <span class="italic">t</span> is the time taken to discharge in seconds (calculated from the discharge curves), <span class="italic">m</span> is the weight of the active electrode (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) in g, and <span class="italic">V</span> is the voltage window (V). The discharge/charge capacities for the sodium battery were expressed in mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>.<p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">For a hybrid cell, in order to maintain the charge conservation between the two electrodes, the mass ratio was calculated using <a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3"><span class="italic">m</span><small><sup>+</sup></small>/<span class="italic">m</span><small><sup></sup></small> = (SC<small><sub></sub></small>  <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub></sub></small>)/(SC<small><sub>+</sub></small>  <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>+</sub></small>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">m</span> represents the mass in g. SC<small><sub></sub></small> and SC<small><sub>+</sub></small> represent the specific capacitance for the AC and MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively (calculated from <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>); <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub></sub></small> and <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>+</sub></small> are the discharge/charge potential range for the AC and MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrodes, respectively.<p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The specific capacitance of the AC and MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> after subtracting from the contributions of acetylene black and binder was calculated to be 200 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (for activated carbon) and 125 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (for MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>). Based on the single electrode characteristics, from the above <a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>, the optimal mass ratio between AC and MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was determined to be 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">0.375. For a mass of 15 mg MMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> material, the mass of the AC was 5.62 mg.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect420"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect422"><span class="b_heading">Physical characterization of plate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> crystallites</span></h3>
        <div>
          <span id="sect425"><span class="c_heading_indent">X-ray diffraction (XRD) studies. </span>
          <span>The structure and phase purity of the as-synthesized MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> samples were examined by X-ray diffraction (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>). All the diffraction peaks matched the standard XRD pattern of the magnesium molybdate crystal (reported in JCPDS 0210961, <span class="italic">a</span> = 10.281 , <span class="italic">b</span> = 9.291 , <span class="italic">c</span> = 7.030 ). The characteristic peaks located at different 2<span class="italic"></span> values were assigned to the respective planes as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a> and this confirms that the chosen combustion synthesis at a low temperature (300 C) is sufficient to synthesise the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> compound. No secondary phases or impurities were observed in the diffraction pattern. The broad diffraction peaks indicate the nanosized particles of the synthesized material. The obtained low signal to-noise ratio is not limited by the XRD itself but due to the chosen synthesis.</span>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanoplates.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect439"><span class="c_heading_indent">Field emission and transmission electron microscopy (FESEM and TEM) studies. </span>
          <span>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were employed to examine the morphology and structure of the as-synthesized MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> samples. Low magnification imaging (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>) showed that the individual particles were fused into rounded clusters with a diameter of several microns with voids illustrating the formation of pores. High magnification imaging (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b and c</a>) showed that individual particles consist of numerous plate-shaped particles of lengths up to a few nm with extensive porosity. The porosity most likely arises from the release of gases (like NH<small><sub>3</sub></small>, CO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) during the decomposition of precursors and urea as fuel through combustion synthesis. It is evident from SEM that clusters of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> particles are closely connected to each other to form a continuous network-like structure that is a pre-requisite for an efficient utilization of the active electrode material, eventually contributing to high storage. The corresponding elemental dispersive X-ray spectra (EDS in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>) show that the analysed area is mainly composed of Mg, Mo, C, and O.</span>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Low and (bc) high magnification SEM images of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> illustrating the formation of numerous plate-shaped particles with a porous structure in different locations. (d) EDS analysis showing the elemental composition of Mg, Mo, C and O.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
          <p class="otherpara">
            <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3ac</a> show low magnification STEM images. The HAADF image in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> shows contrast which is a function of thickness multiplied by atomic number squared. This produces very strong contrast between the molybdate phase and thin carbon support film. The BF image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>) shows diffraction contrast from the crystalline particles. However, the thickness of the largest clusters obscures the details of the individual crystallites. The SE image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>) shows the topographic structure of the particles  producing information analogous to that obtained from SE imaging in an SEM  albeit at much higher resolution. The MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanoparticles are associated with numerous pores and the pore sizes vary in several nanometers. Therefore, these three images (HAADF, BF, and SE) provide complementary structural insights. EDS mapping combined with phase analysis using principal component analysis (PCA) showed that the molybdate was a single phase, in agreement with XRD (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3df</a> show HAADF, BF and SE details of the region in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>. It shows an ensemble of small plate-like crystals (of around 50 nm side length) stacked obliquely. These are clustered on the surface of larger MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> plates. The stacked individual plate-like particles are clearly visible in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3h and i</a>, exhibiting a hierarchical architecture. High-resolution imaging of a thin plate appearance of around 10 nm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3j</a>) and a detailed BF image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3k</a>) confirm the highly crystalline structure. The electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) analysis of this region shows (Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) a small amount of carbon present in the molybdate framework. However, mottling in the HAADF image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3j</a>) and bright field contrast at lower magnification (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3l</a>) reveal an extensive network of fine (<span class="italic">ca.</span> 10 nm) pores in the crystals.</p>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a, d, g, j and k) HAADF STEM images, (b, e, h and l) BF images, and (c, f and i) SE images of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>. (ac) Low magnification images, (df) plate-shaped twinned particles. (gi) Typical MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> showing nanoplate hierarchical architecture. (j) Thin overlapping plates, (k) high magnification of the plate showing a high degree of crystallinity with weak mottling due to fine scale porosity (l) 525 nm pores in MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> plates.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
          <p class="otherpara">The microscopy results show that a nanoplate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> hierarchical architecture with considerable porosity has been successfully synthesized. The electrochemical performance of the material is heavily influenced by the pore size distribution, and therefore, the porosity of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> samples was measured by the BrunauerEmmettTeller (BET) and BarrettJoynerHalenda (BJH) analyses and the results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a>. The samples possess well-developed porosity, reflected by the N<small><sub>2</sub></small> sorption isotherm of type III,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> having a BET surface area of 25.2 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. From the adsorption branch BJH method, the porous structure consists of two main pore size distributions. This hierarchical structure consists of large pores, with a pore size range between 50 and 70 nm and on the walls of these pores, there are much smaller pores of around 10 nm. The hierarchical pore structure is likely to be highly advantageous to the electrochemical performance of the resulting electrode since it will facilitate the ion diffusion rate.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1517</span></sup></a></p>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherm and inset showing BJH adsorption pore size distribution of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect486"><span class="c_heading_indent">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) studies. </span>
          <span>To ascertain the electronic state of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> sample, an X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) study was carried out and the results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>. XPS is a surface technique; the photoelectrons emitted from the sample surface have a kinetic energy that is specific to the element from which they originate, and the local bonding environment. Therefore, the signals indicate elemental composition and electronic (oxidation) state information. The wide scan spectra of the as-synthesized MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> sample (Fig. S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) display the main elements (Mg 2p, Mo 3d, O 1s and C 1s). The ratios of Mo<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">O were in good agreement with what is expected for molybdates (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">4). The atomic percentages of the elements calculated from the wide spectrum are found to be Mg (13.3%), Mo (13.3%), O (52.0), C (20.7%) and S (0.7%). The narrow scan spectra for the elements Mg 1s, Mo 3d, C 1s and O 1s are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5ae</a>. The Mg 1s and KLL peak (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>) are centred at 1303.8 eV (binding energy) and 1181.8 eV (kinetic energy), respectively, which is in good agreement with the reported value in the XPS database.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> The doublet at 231 eV and 234.1 eV in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a> is attributed to Mo 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> and Mo 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> The Mo 3d region was fitted with two doublets, one doublet with an asymmetric LF(1.3, 5, 55, 300) line shape for Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> and one doublet with a symmetric GL(30) at lower binding energy resulting due to the X-ray induced reduction of the Mo. While the Mo 3p (in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>) obscures the presence of an N 1s peak, a possible fit to this region is presented and the remaining area is then fitted with a single N 1s peak centred at 400 eV attributed to C-NH<small><sub>2</sub></small> from urea as a fuel. C 1s spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>) were fitted with three components at 284.6285 eV, 286.3286.7 eV, and 288.6 eV and assigned to CC, CO, and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O, respectively. The former component could be likely due to the adventitious hydrocarbon contamination while the other two may be attributed to residual urea from the synthesis. The O 1s components (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>) were fitted to two components, one calibrated for the metal oxide at 530.45 eV and the other for carbonoxygen species, around 532 eV. The results of the XPS are consistent with the XRD results and indicate that the phase pure material is also found at the surface and has been obtained through the combustion synthesis.</span>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>. High resolution spectra for regions (a) Mg 1s, (b) Mo 3d, (c) Mo 3p, (d) C 1s, and (e) O 1s.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        </span></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect508"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical characterization of plate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> crystallites for capacitor applications</span></h3>
        <span>The hybrid capacitor, coupling magnesium molybdate (MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) with activated carbon, is a potential candidate to enhance the electrochemical properties of capacitors. To evaluate this electrochemically and to understand the unrevealed redox mechanism, initially, MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was tested as a single electrode and then fabricated as a device to examine its suitability for energy storage devices. To understand the effect of replacing aqueous 2 M NaOH with non-aqueous 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC in a MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>|AC cell, we have carried out experiments in aqueous and non-aqueous electrolytes. The specific capacitance of each electrode of the hybrid cell was evaluated by potentiostatic and galvanostatic modes and the profiles are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">7</a>.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) First cyclic voltammetric (CV) curves, and (b) chargedischarge behaviour (three-electrode configuration) of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode. (c) CV curves (three-electrode configuration) of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as positive compared with activated carbon as the negative electrode. Hybrid cell (two-electrode configuration) (d) CV and (e) chargedischarge curves of supercapacitors comprising ACMgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in 2 M NaOH aqueous electrolyte. Both CV and chargedischarge curves show pseudocapacitive behaviour, (f) Nyquist plot comparing the impedance in a high frequency region and capacitive behaviour in a low frequency region for single and hybrid cells. Sweep rates and current density are indicated in the respective figures.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) First CV curves (two-electrode configuration) of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as negative compared with activated carbon as the positive electrode <span class="italic">versus</span> metallic sodium in non-aqueous 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC (88<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2) electrolyte. Hybrid cell (two-electrode configuration) (b) CV and (c) chargedischarge curves of supercapacitor comprising ACMgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the non-aqueous electrolyte. Both CV and chargedischarge curves show pseudocapacitive behaviour, (cd) chargedischarge curves for consecutive cycles (f) Nyquist plot comparing the impedance in a high frequency region and capacitive behaviour in a low frequency region for (a) single and (b) hybrid cells. Sweep rates and cycles numbers are indicated in the respective figures.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect529"><span class="b_heading">Aqueous electrolyte</span></h3>
        <div>
          <span id="sect531"><span class="c_heading_indent">Single cell characteristics  MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> and activated carbon. </span>
          <span>
            <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a</a> shows the comparison of cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves using MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as an electrode in a three-electrode configuration at different sweep rates in 2 M NaOH aqueous electrolyte. The electrode was measured with a potential window of 0.6 V and all the curves exhibit a quasi-rectangular shape, indicating the typical characteristics of asymmetric capacitive behaviour. No clear peaks corresponding to oxidation or reduction are seen in the CV profile. The calculated specific capacitances from the cyclic voltammetry curves are 207, 188, 177, and 150 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at a sweep rate of 1, 2, 3 and 5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The capacitance values were determined by integrating the area under the CV curve as a function of sweep rate. The observed trend indicates a diffusion-controlled redox reaction. The electrochemical behaviour of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in aqueous NaOH is associated with the electron-transfer mechanism involving the ability of OH<small><sup></sup></small> to be adsorbed and desorbed reversibly. It may also be reversibly intercalated into the oxidised form of Mg(OH)<small><sub>2</sub></small> reversibly for the faradaic process forming MgOOH, which could be attributed to the pseudo-capacitance. Since Mg is an alkaline earth metal, which exhibits oxidation states of zero (metal) and two (in all Mg compounds), the redox reactions of binary metal oxides like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> can be attributed to the other Mo cations, since Mo possesses multiple oxidation states. However, it has been reported<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> that in alkaline solutions <span class="italic">i.e.</span> NaOH of around pH 9, the molybdate species exist only as MoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>. However, in the presence of excess OH<small><sup></sup></small> ions, it forms as Mo(OH)<small><sub>3</sub></small> prior to any electrochemical measurements. Mo(OH)<small><sub>3</sub></small> does not exhibit any redox peaks in the pH range 712,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> so the only plausible mechanism could be OH<small><sup></sup></small> ions forming a double layer on the surface of Mg(OH)<small><sub>2</sub></small> and intercalating into Mg sites forming MgOOH. Based on this, we can see that the curves are reversible and stable within the given potential window contributing to 200 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> specific capacitance. The chargedischarge behaviour of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was investigated at four different current densities with an identical voltage window of 0.6 V. Typical voltage profiles are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>. The material showed a near-symmetric behaviour upon reversing the polarity, indicating a good reversibility. A triangular shape without any significant ohmic drop or plateau like a curve indicates good charge transport and a material which is capacitive in nature. The calculated discharge capacitances for the chargedischarge curves are very similar to those obtained from the CV curves, with a high capacitance of 200 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Therefore, MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> is a good candidate for a positive electrode. Conventional activated carbon (AC) has been used for negative electrodes; the typical curves for single electrode AC and MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> are compared in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a>. The shape of the CV curve for the AC electrode in the negative potential range of 1.0 V exhibits a nearly ideal rectangular shape, which is a clear representation of electrochemical double layer (EDL) capacitor behaviour.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> A value of 125 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> was calculated for the negative electrode of activated carbon, derived from the non-faradaic mechanism. The behaviour and potential range of AC differ from those of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a>. The AC is capacitive in nature while the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> behaves like a pseudocapacitive type material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> Overall, the operating voltage for this hybrid cell could be 1.6 V. Although the area of the CV curve for AC is larger than that of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, the oxide has an extended voltage window compared to the positive electrode. In order to ensure the charge balance of the hybrid cell, the weight ratio between the negative and positive electrodes was 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">0.375 (<a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>).</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          <span id="sect568"><span class="c_heading_indent">Hybrid cell characteristics  MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small><span class="italic">vs.</span> activated carbon. </span>
          <span>The typical CV of a hybrid cell is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>. The hybrid cell was cycled between 0 and 1.6 V at various current densities and exhibited an excellent reversibility. The observed integral part of the curve increases when increasing the scan rate, attributed to the increased mass rate. At a higher scan rate (5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>), the anodic peak potential shifted in the positive direction (+0.4 V; as indicated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>) suggesting that higher potentials are needed for the same product to occur. Notably, the more the separation between the oxidation and reduction peaks during the forward and reverse scans, the more irreversible is the electrode process at a very high scan rate.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> Among the obtained CV profiles at various current densities, higher sweep rates (3 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>) show a clear faradaic current but have a broad reduction peak. The calculated specific capacitances from the cyclic voltammetry curves are 52, 43, 34, and 25 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at a sweep rate of 1, 2, 3 and 5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Clearly, a pair of peaks is observed during the oxidation and subsequent reduction, indicating that the electrochemical processes include both faradaic (pseudocapacitance) and non-faradaic (double layer capacitance) mechanisms, therefore it is a pseudocapacitive material. Testing in galvanic mode resulted in a very similar behaviour, with distorted triangular-shaped chargedischarge curves (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6e</a>). Undoubtedly, this excellent performance stems from the hierarchical plate-like structure of the material. The Nyquist plot of the single and hybrid cell has been compared in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6f</a>. In the high frequency region, the internal resistance is quite low for both the cells and hence the observed low IR drop in the galvanostatic plots (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b and e</a>) and there is not much difference seen in charge transfer resistance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6e</a>). However, in the low frequency region, the vertical line indicates a capacitive behaviour, which is more pronounced for the single cell than the hybrid.</span>
        </span></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect585"><span class="b_heading">Non-aqueous electrolyte</span></h3>
        <span>
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a> shows comparative cyclic voltammetric curves for MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> and AC in the non-aqueous electrolyte, tested in the two-electrode configuration using metallic sodium as an anode. The specific capacitance for the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> and AC measured in the two-electrode system within the potential window of 2.0 V was calculated to be 64 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 20 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Interestingly, a reversible capacitance of 60 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is observed for MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the half-cell (two-electrode) configuration, which is about three times lower than that observed for the aqueous electrolyte. The CV curve of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> exhibits a well-defined reduction (<span class="italic">C</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> = 0.58 V) and oxidation peak (<span class="italic">A</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> = 0.85 V), corresponding to the faradaic redox reactions, Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small>  Mo<small><sup>4+</sup></small> + 2e<small><sup></sup></small>, which is a characteristic of pseudocapacitive behaviour.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> The CV shape of the AC in the potential range of 2 V is quite different from that of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> pseudocapacitive behaviour because the overall capacitance of the AC arises solely from the EDL.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> A near-rectangular shape with no obvious redox peaks is an indication of EDL behavior. The charge balance of the hybrid cell between the negative and positive electrodes was 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">3.2. Therefore, the sodium ion capacitor using a non-aqueous solution comprises an intercalation anode and a surface adsorption cathode where Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and ClO<small><sub>4</sub></small><small><sup>_</sup></small> counter ions act as charge carriers. The typical CV curve of the hybrid cell is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a>. A quasi-rectangular shaped CV with a peak-like shoulder is visible, indicating that the carbon electrode will reversibly adsorb ClO<small><sub>4</sub></small><small><sup></sup></small> and release Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> while MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> involves the pseudocapacitive redox interaction of Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ions. The resulting capacitance of 34 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is therefore achieved both by EDL and pseudocapacitance.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">With increasing scan rate to 6 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the cell displayed negligible shape distortion of the original CV, which shows that the material is desirable for fast discharge/charge in power applications. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7ce</a> show the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles for various current rates and cycling stability at 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The specific capacitances of the hybrid cell are 38, 35, 32, and 30 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 1.2, 1.4, 1.6 and 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. This suggests that the electrodes are sustainable at a higher current rate, however the curves are asymmetrical with high IR drops. The shape of the chargedischarge curves for the consecutive cycles is also shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7d</a>. In contrast, at a lower current rate of 0.2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the curves (in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7e</a>) are symmetrical with a low IR drop, an improved capacitance of 45 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and excellent reversibility for multiple cycles. The Nyquist plots of the half-cell configuration and hybrid cell are compared in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7f</a>. In the high frequency region between 0 and 200 Z<small><sup>1</sup></small>, a clear semicircle is observed followed by a weakly rising trend in the intermediate to low frequency region. Compared to the hybrid cell, a smaller semicircle is observed for half-cell (single) configuration indicating a low interfacial charge-transfer resistance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> Thereafter, the trend rises almost vertically, which is indicative of the good capacitive performance of the fabricated device. Therefore, the superior electrochemical performance with excellent storage retention and reversibility of the aqueous electrolyte can be attributed to the higher ionic conductivity and fast electron kinetics.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The primary objective of the current work is to enhance the energy and power densities of the supercapacitor. To demonstrate the operational characteristics of the two systems evaluated here, the energy density, the Ragone plot, and cycling stability are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8</a>. The energy density plots of the hybrid cell at various current densities are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a</a>. A high energy density value (250 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) obtained for the non-aqueous system has been significantly greater than those reported for the hybrid sodium-ion capacitors tested in organic NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrolyte.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,24</span></sup></a> Even when tested at a higher value of current density (2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), the energy density is retained to 135 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. In the case of an aqueous system tested here, the energy density of 60 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small> was four times lower than the non-aqueous counterpart. Nevertheless, the retention rate was higher than the non-aqueous system and comparable to those of the values reported in the literature for cobalt and nickel molybdates tested in aqueous electrolytes.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,26</span></sup></a> The lower value of energy density in aqueous systems results from the 2 V cell limit to avoid the decomposition of water. The Ragone plot of the hybrid cell (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8b</a>) in the non-aqueous electrolyte shows that a high energy density of 250 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small> is achieved at a power density of 1500 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Even at a high power density of 9720 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the device still has an energy density of 135 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. This performance is superior to that of aqueous supercapacitors tested here (5760 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 32 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and to that of several hybrid materials, including hierarchical structures, reported in the literature.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2730</span></sup></a> These results demonstrate the importance and great potential application of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> plate-like architectures<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> in the development of energy storage systems. The cycling stability of the hybrid cell (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8c</a>) is further evaluated at a constant current density of 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for 1000 cycles and obviously the aqueous system shows much improved performance with higher capacitance retention compared to the non-aqueous system. The differences in the mechanisms through which MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> undergoes electrochemical processes in aqueous NaOH and non-aqueous NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> can be explained in terms of the formation of the product such as MgOOH through OH<small><sup></sup></small> ions adsorption and desorption properties. This may be attributed to the higher ionic conductivity and faster alkali ion transport in aqueous systems. The results in both electrolytes are encouraging but the cycling stability and capacitance retention need to be significantly improved for the non-aqueous system to be suitable for practical power devices.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">Power performance characteristics of hybrid supercapacitor ACMgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (a) energy density plot, (b) Ragone plot, and (c) cycling stability in aqueous and non-aqueous electrolytes.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect659"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical characterization of plate-like MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> crystallites for battery applications</span></h3>
        <span>MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was also tested as an anode for sodium ion batteries. The CV profile of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was recorded in the voltage of 0.0052.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na at a sweep rate of 2 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9a</a>). The characteristics of the first cycle are quite different from those of the subsequent cycles. The first sweep was initiated in the cathodic direction and then reversed back anodically to 2.5 V. While two peaks C<small><sub>1</sub></small> and C<small><sub>2</sub></small> at 1.1 and 0.45 V are visible in the first reduction, one broad peak at A<small><sub>1</sub></small> at 0.9 is observed in the subsequent oxidation. In the subsequent cycles, the cathodic peaks C<small><sub>1</sub></small> and C<small><sub>2</sub></small> shifted to lower potentials to 0.68 and 0.21 with reduced peak currents while the anodic peak A<small><sub>1</sub></small> remained unchanged. The cathodic reduction of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> gave rise to three distinct materials as shown in <a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a>, such as MgO, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and the oxidation of these materials resulted in the original MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> material (<a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a>). The second reduction profile showed that these peaks shifted to lower potentials corresponding to the formation of MgO and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>3</sub></small> (<a title="" href="#eqn6">eqn (6)</a>) which is reversible and the subsequent profiles are qualitatively similar. Overall, the shape of the subsequent discharge and charge curves is almost identical in the second and fifth cycles, though the discharge curves are not exactly superimposable as the charge process. This minor discrepancy could be attributed to the stabilization process of the electrode that depends on the quality of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer formed on the surface.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> The electrochemistry of the redox mechanism and the formation of materials formed in sodium-ion batteries for molybdates have not been well proposed in the literature yet.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig9"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr03824d-f9.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr03824d/c8nr03824d-f9.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 9 </b> <span id="fig9"><span class="graphic_title">(a) First five CV curves (two-cell configuration) of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small><span class="italic">versus</span> metallic sodium in non-aqueous 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DMC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">FEC (88<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">10<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2) electrolyte. (b) Chargedischarge behaviour, and (c) rate capability of the sodium battery (NaMgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>). Cycle numbers and current rates are indicated in the respective figures. The letters C<small><sub>1</sub></small>, C<small><sub>2</sub></small> and A<small><sub>1</sub></small> refer to reduction and oxidation peaks, respectively. The terms A and B are boundary markings for the products formed during discharge, the region A is reversible.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The storage and cycling properties of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> were investigated by galvanostatic cycling with the voltage window of 0.0052.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na at a current of C/10 rate and the profiles are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9b</a>. The shape of the first discharge profile (labelled 1) is characterized by an initial drop in voltage (from open-circuit voltage OCV 2.4 V) to 1.0 V, followed by a long plateau at 0.8 V and then a long downward sloping potential profile until the cut off voltage. The MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode produced a first discharge capacity of 580 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> as a cathode material in a sodium-ion battery, corresponding to the insertion of 4 Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> equivalents per mole of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (1 mol of Na corresponds to the capacity of 145 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). Upon charging (up to 2.5 V), the cell voltage increased roughly linearly with time to 1.2 V and a gradual rise to 2.5 V is seen. The initial discharge and charge capacities are 590 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 292 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The observed irreversible capacity is due to the formation of a passivation layer, called the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), on the surface of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode during the initial cycles.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> This layer protects the electrolyte from further loss of capacity in the successive cycles. The discharge profile in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9b</a> has been divided into regions marked with A and B and we proposed a reaction mechanism, involving the formation of various compounds during the redox reactions. The amorphization of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> and a long plateau at 0.8 V in region A consume 2 mol of Na (about 292 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after subtracting the C and binder content), which is reversible. At location B, MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> has converted to irreversible Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O consuming another 2 mol. of Na, in total 590 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, as per <a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a>.</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4">MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> + 4Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 4e<small><sup></sup></small>  MgO + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5">MgO + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>2</sub></small>  MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> + 4Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 4e<small><sup></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn6"><span id="eqn6">MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> + 2Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2e<small><sup></sup></small>  MgO + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>3</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(6)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The charge curve corresponds to the formation of products shown in <a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a>, which reforms the starting material. The subsequent dischargecharge curves correspond to <a title="" href="#eqn6">eqn (6)</a>, which is equivalent to 2 mol of Na insertion of reversible capacity 292 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The Mg<small><sup>2+</sup></small> ion in MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> is not expected to be reduced to Mg metal under room-temperature electrochemical conditions. The charge and discharge capacities of the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9b</a>) were stable at approximately 250 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and no significant capacity loss was observed for the first 75 cycles. The rate capability of the electrode was cycle tested at various current densities ranging from 0.04 to 0.32 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and the plots are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9c</a>. When the current density was increased stepwise from 0.04 to 0.08, 0.16 and 0.32 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and tested for ten cycles, the average discharge capacity was found to be 210, 135, 90, and 70 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Upon reverting the current density back to 0.04 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, an average discharge capacity as high as 175 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> could be recovered. Based on these results, we suggest that the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanoplates with hierarchical architecture are strong anode candidates among the available conventional hard carbon materials<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> of sodium-ion batteries because of the excellent cyclability, reversibility and rate capability.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect757"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was successfully synthesized by the combustion route. Microscopy results confirmed that the morphology was plate-like and the structure was hierarchical in nature. X-ray diffraction and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy confirmed that despite the low temperature route used for synthesis, the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> phase was obtained with good crystallinity and with all elements in their expected valence states. The highly porous, high surface area phase makes it a potential candidate for energy storage devices like supercapacitors and rechargeable batteries in sodium based systems. Fast charge storage and cycling properties of MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrodes were examined against conventional activated carbon and in a hybrid device. The effect of electrolyte, both aqueous and non-aqueous, was also investigated. The assembled hybrid cell in an aqueous electrolyte exhibited a specific capacitance of 43 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, whereas, in the non-aqueous system for the same current density, the available capacitance reduced to 30 F g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. This could be attributed to the highly conducting aqueous electrolyte enabling conductive paths for electron transfer and efficient ion access, thus the improved performance. The capacitance retention was found to be higher (over 95% after 1000 cycles) in aqueous electrolytes. Notably, the aqueous cell was limited by the 2 V voltage window and hence produced lower energy and power density. The high power performance of the non-aqueous system suggests that there is potential as an electrode for the sodium-ion capacitor. When MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> was used as an anode material for a sodium-ion battery, it delivered a specific discharge capacity of 590 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. This is equivalent to 4 mol of Na insertion, only 2 mol of which is reversible. The battery showed excellent cycling stability with no significant loss in capacity and exhibited high rate capability. After 75 cycles, the MgMoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode retained 225 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The chargedischarge mechanisms are proposed for sodium ion batteries.</span>
